The Host Analogy and the Argument from Grace
Supplementary analogies: host and stinginess argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the supplementary analogies of the host and the stinginess argument that reinforce why God must provide the most effective grace (lutf).

Core Idea
Withholding beneficial grace when possible is comparable to stinginess and foolishness.

The Mu'tazilites provide further examples to solidify their argument. The host analogy shows that a wise person must use gentle invitation to ensure attendance, otherwise he is foolish. Then they argue that if God withholds grace that benefits the servant without harming Himself, He would be like a stingy, foolish, and cruel person—which is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the host analogy
Read the example of a man preparing a banquet and inviting a guest. Notice that if he knows a cheerful invitation will work, he must use it; otherwise he is not wise.
2
Identify the parallel to God
The analogy is applied to God: He must use the most inviting means (lutf) to bring servants to obedience, otherwise He would be unwise.
3
Examine the stinginess argument
Read the argument that if God withholds grace despite having the power and no harm, He would be considered stingy and foolish—attributes impossible for God.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with the affirmation that God is free from such negative attributes, reinforcing the necessity of al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا كان له عدو يدعوه إلى موالاته ويجب رجوعه إلى طاعته فلن يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما يعلم أنه أنجع فيما يريد منه، وأدعى له إلى ترك ما هو فيه من عداوته، فإن عرض له أمران من الشدة والغلظة والملاينة والملاطفة يعلم أن أحدهما أدعى لعدوه إلى المراجعة والإنابة، والآخر دون ذلك ففعل الأدون وترك أن يفعل الأصلح الأدعى وكلاهما في قدرته عليهما بمنزلة لا يضره بذلهما ولا ينفعه منعهما، كان عند الحكماء جميعاً مذموماً خارجاً عن استحقاق الوصف بالجود والحكمة، فلما كان هذا فيما بينا على ما وصفنا، وكان الله عز وجل قادرا رحيما جواداً عالمًا بمواضع حاجة عباده آمرا لهم بطاعته وترك عداوته والرجوع إلى ولايته لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع ولا يلحقه منه ذم، علمنا أنه لا يفعل بهم إلا أصلح الأشياء لهم في دينهم وأدعى لهم إلى طاعته سقما كان ذلك أو صحة لذة أو ألماً، آمنوا أو كفروا، أطاعوا أو عصوا. قال الله تعالى: وَبَلَوْنَهُم بِالحَسَنَاتِ وَالسَّيِّئَاتِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَرْجِعُونَ (٢) وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِّن نَّبِيِّ إِلَّا أَخَذْنَا أَهْلَهَا بِالْبَأْسَاءِ وَالضَّرَّاءِ ) (٣). دل ذلك أنه يعاملهم بمختلف الأحوال(4) على ما يرى الأصلح لهم والأدعى إلى الحق، وربما يوضحون هذا بمن أنجز ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملايته لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر، يجب عليه أن يدعوه ببشر وملاطفة ولو عامله بضد ذلك لما كان حكيمًا، وصار منع ذلك بمنزلة منع التمكين من الحضور، ومنع التمكين عن فعل ما أمره به وذا ليس بحكمة بل هو سفه فكذا هذا، وربما يقولون لو أعطى العبد ما في مقدوره من اللطف انتفع به ولم يتضرر الله تعالى ولو لم يعطه لتضرر العبد وما انتفع الله تعالى بالمنع، ومن منع غيره ما لو أعطى لانتفع به غيره ولو لم يعط لتضرر به غيره، ولا ضرر على المانع بالإعطاء، ولا منفعة له بالمنع لعُدَّ نهاية في البخل والسفه، والقساوة والله منزه عن الوصف بهذه الصفات.
And likewise, if one has an enemy whom he calls to his allegiance and whose return to obedience is obligatory, he may not treat him with harshness or gentleness except with what he knows is most effective in what he wants from him, and most likely to make him abandon the enmity he is in. For if two matters present themselves to him—severity and harshness versus softness and gentleness—and he knows that one of them is more likely to make his enemy return and repent, while the other is inferior, and he does the inferior and omits to do the more beneficial and more compelling, while both are within his power and are such that neither giving them harms him nor withholding them benefits him—then he would be universally blamed by the wise, falling outside the description of generosity and wisdom. Since this is as we have described, and since Allah Almighty is powerful, merciful, generous, knowing the places of need of His servants, commanding them to obey Him and abandon enmity and return to His allegiance—and giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, nor does any blame attach to Him—we know that He does not do with them except the most beneficial of things for their religion and the most compelling for them to obey Him, whether that be sickness or health, pleasure or pain, whether they believe or disbelieve, obey or disobey. Allah Almighty said: 'And We tested them with good things and evil so that they might return' (7:168), and He said: 'And We did not send a prophet to any town but We seized its people with adversity and hardship' (7:94). That indicates that He treats them with varying conditions according to what He sees as most beneficial for them and most compelling to the truth. Sometimes they clarify this with the example of a man who prepared a banquet for another and ordered him to attend, desiring that, and knew that if he invited him cheerfully and gently he would attend and his purpose would be achieved, but if he did so with harshness and frowning he would not attend—then he must invite him cheerfully and gently, and if he treated him otherwise he would not be wise. And preventing that is like preventing the enabling of attendance, and preventing the enabling of doing what he commanded is not wisdom but foolishness. So too is this. Sometimes they say: If God gave the servant what is within His power of grace, the servant would benefit and God would not be harmed; and if He did not give it, the servant would be harmed and God would not benefit from withholding. And whoever withholds from another what, if given, would benefit the other, and if not given would harm the other, while the giver suffers no harm in giving nor gain in withholding, is counted as extreme in stinginess and foolishness and cruelty. And Allah is free from being described with such attributes.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument for the doctrine of al-aslah, i.e., that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. The author begins with an analogy: if a person has an enemy whom he calls to allegiance and obedience, a wise person would use the most effective means—whether harsh or gentle—to achieve the desired return, choosing the superior option if both are available and neither harms him. If he chooses the inferior, he is blamed by the wise as lacking generosity and wisdom. Then the author applies the analogy to God: since God is powerful, merciful, generous, and knows His servants' needs, and since giving does not harm Him nor withholding benefit Him, He must do only the most beneficial for them in religion, compelling them to obedience through various conditions (health, sickness, pleasure, pain, belief, disbelief, obedience, disobedience). Two Quranic verses are cited to support that God tests people with good and evil to lead them back. The passage then provides a second analogy: a host who invites a guest knows that a cheerful invitation will bring the guest, but a harsh one will not; thus a wise host must invite cheerfully. Similarly, God must provide the best means (lutf) to guide His servants. If He withholds it, He would be like a stingy, foolish, and cruel person, but God is free from such attributes. The overall argument is that if God does not provide the most effective grace to bring His servants to obedience, He would be acting contrary to wisdom and justice, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
اللطف (al-lutf)
البخل
السفه
القساوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can withhold grace arbitrarily without affecting His wisdom.
Correction: The argument shows that withholding grace when beneficial is inconsistent with wisdom, generosity, and justice.

Checkpoint
In the host analogy, why must the host invite cheerfully?
A) Because the guest deserves it.
B) Because otherwise the guest might come anyway.
C) Because it is the only way to achieve his purpose, and doing otherwise would be unwise.
D) Because the host is generous.

Key Takeaway
Withholding beneficial grace when possible is incompatible with God's perfect wisdom and generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's grace is sufficient for you, or do you sometimes feel He has withheld what you need to obey? How does this passage challenge your view? Will you change how you think about your own failures?
?
When you see someone failing to obey God, do you consider that God may have not provided them with the grace they needed? How should this affect your judgment of them? Will you become more merciful?
?
This passage compares withholding grace to stinginess and cruelty. Is there any area in your life where you are stingy with God's grace—perhaps in forgiving or helping others? What concrete step will you take today to be generous?

Refuting the Claim of Beneficial Pre-emptive Death
The author uses an analogy to refute the claim that causing death before apostasy is more beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses analogy to refute the claim that it is more beneficial for God to cause an apostate's death before apostasy.

Core Idea
Forcing death before apostasy is like sending a son to certain ruin.

This passage addresses a nuanced theological argument about divine foreknowledge and human benefit. The opponent claims that since God knows someone will apostatize, it would be better to cause their death beforehand. The author responds with a devastating analogy that exposes the absurdity of this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the sarcastic tone in the beginning.
2
Identify the initial reasoning
The author first implicitly rejects the claim by stating that God gives intellect and guidance, making life an opportunity for higher reward.
This sets up his positive case.
3
Examine the analogy
The detailed analogy of a father sending his son on a trading journey despite knowing he will be robbed and killed illustrates the irrationality of the opponent's position.
Consider how the analogy mirrors the divine scenario.
4
Understand the dilemma
The author presents a dilemma: if the opponent says the father is acting beneficially, he is foolish; if not, his argument collapses.
This forces the opponent into an untenable position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من يعلم الله تعالى أنه يرتد لا محالة أصلح له من الإماتة قبل وقت الارتداد لما أنه تعالى ركب فيه العقل وأمره بالإيمان وهداه السبيل وبين حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفر، فكان إبقاؤه إياه مع هذه المعاني تعريضا له لأعلى المنزلتين وأسناهما، ولإحراز زيادة الثواب والتعريض لذلك مصلحة له، ومن المعقول الذي لا ريب فيه أن هذا الكلام لو تكلم به مبرسم أو هذى به مخبل لتعجب منه السامعون وضحك منه الحاضرون، ثم يقال له: لو أن رجلاً دفع مالاً إلى ولد له وأمره أن يذهب إلى بلده كذا ويتجر، وكان الأمر بحال لو وصل الابن إلى تلك البلدة وأتجر بها لوقعت له تجارات رابحة وحصلت له أموال نفيسة جمة، غير أن الأب يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يصل إلى تلك البلدة بل يقطع عليه الطريق ويغار على ما دفعه إليه من الأموال ويجز رقبته، أو يعلم يقينا أن ابنه لا يتجر بتلك الأموال بل يقامر بها ينفقها في ثمن الشراب وأجرة الزواني والقحاب ثم مع علمه بهذا يدفع إليه الأموال ويأمره بالذهاب ويحرضه على التجارة وينهاه عما يصده عنها، أيكون هذا الرجل بهذا الصنيع والتعريض للتجارة الرابحة فاعلاً بهذا الولد ما هو الأصلح ومريدا لصلاحه وصلاح ماله مع علمه بما يؤول إليه عاقبة أمره؟ فإن قال: كان بذلك فاعلاً به الأصلح ومريدًا لصلاحه. نادى على نفسه بالحمق والمكابرة بل بعدم العقل ويسخر به كل من سمع ذلك منه، وإذا آل الأمر إلى ارتكاب مثل هذا المحال كان الإعراض عن مجادلته أصوب وتصوير عقيدته لعامة الناس ليعرفوا عناده أو غباوته أوجب، وإن قال: لم يكن بهذا التعريض فاعلاً به الأصلح ولا مريدًا لصلاحه. فقد أبطل كلامه ولزمته الحجة.
He whom God knows will certainly apostatize, it is more beneficial for him to die before the time of apostasy? Because God invested in him intellect, commanded him to believe, guided him to the path, and made clear the goodness of faith and evil of disbelief. So allowing him to continue with these meanings is exposing him to the highest of the two stations and their noblest, and to attain increase of reward, and exposure to that is beneficial for him. And it is among what is rational with no doubt that if this speech were uttered by a madman or raved by a confounded one, the listeners would marvel at him and those present would laugh at him. Then it is said to him: If a man gave money to his son and commanded him to go to such-and-such town and trade, and the situation was such that if the son reached that town and traded there, he would have profitable trades and get many valuable monies, but the father knows for certain that his son will not reach that town, rather he will be waylaid and robbed of the money given to him and his neck will be cut, or he knows for certain that his son will not trade with that money but rather gamble with it, spending it on the price of wine and the wages of prostitutes and whores, then with his knowledge of this he gives him the money and commands him to go and urges him to trade and forbids him from what would hinder him from it. Is this man by this action and exposure to the profitable trade doing what is more beneficial to this son and intending his welfare and the welfare of his money with his knowledge of the final outcome? If he says: By that he is doing the more beneficial and intending his welfare, he proclaims against himself folly and stubbornness, rather lack of intellect, and everyone who hears that from him will mock him. And if the matter leads to undertaking such an absurdity, then turning away from debating him is more correct, and portraying his belief to the common people so that they know his stubbornness or his foolishness is more obligatory. And if he says: He is not by this exposure doing the more beneficial to him nor intending his welfare, then he has nullified his statement and the proof is incumbent upon him.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy against those who claim it is more beneficial (أصلح) for God to cause an apostate to die before apostasy. The author begins by implicitly rejecting that claim: God has given the person intellect, commanded belief, and shown the goodness of faith—so letting him live exposes him to the highest stations and reward. Then he sarcastically notes that even a madman's speech would be laughed at if he said such a thing. He then presents a detailed analogy: a father gives money to his son for a trading journey, knowing the son will be robbed and killed. Any rational person would say the father is not acting in the son's best interest. The opponent is faced with a dilemma: if he says the father is doing the beneficial thing, he is foolish; if he says not, then his own argument about God fails. Thus the opponent's position is refuted.

Key Terms
أصلح
تعريض
مصلحة
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge of future apostasy necessitates that causing death before apostasy is always more beneficial.
Correction: The author shows that such a claim is absurd because God's actions of giving intellect and guidance make continued life an opportunity for greater reward, and the analogy demonstrates the irrationality of the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy used in the passage, if a father sends his son on a journey knowing he will be killed, what does the author conclude?
A) The father is acting in the son's best interest.
B) The father is foolish and not acting beneficially.
C) The father is wise to expose him to profit.
D) The analogy does not apply to God.

Key Takeaway
The analogy shows that acting against knowledge of harm cannot be considered beneficial, so God's allowing apostates to live is actually an opportunity for greater reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone you know will likely fail or sin, do you trust that God's wisdom in allowing them to continue is greater than your own assessment? How does this analogy make you reconsider your attitude toward others' mistakes?
?
The father in the analogy knows the son will ruin himself, yet still gives him the chance. Can you think of a time God gave you an opportunity despite your known weakness? How did that opportunity shape your faith, even if you faltered?
?
If you were the son in the analogy, would you resent your father for sending you into danger, or thank him for the chance to gain? How does this mirror your response to God's trials in your own life?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on the Obligation of Lutf
Refutes Mu'tazila on obligation of lutf through reward logic and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that God must provide lutf is contradictory and how withholding lutf can be more beneficial for the servant's ultimate reward.

Core Idea
Withholding lutf can be more beneficial than giving it.

The Mu'tazila argued that God must provide lutf (divine grace) to enable obedience, making it obligatory upon Him. The author begins by finding a great contradiction in their position. He then shows that performing acts without lutf is actually more rewarding, so withholding it serves the servant's best interest. This lesson unpacks the author's refutation and the powerful analogy he presents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Read the opening sentence to see what the Mu'tazila claim (that God is obligated to provide lutf). Notice the author calls it a great contradiction.
2
Understand the reward logic
Follow the argument: performing an act without lutf is harder and thus more rewarding; with lutf it is easier and less rewarding. Therefore, withholding lutf is more beneficial ('aslah').
This is the key insight that counters the Mu'tazila.
3
Grasp 'higher of the two stations'
The author introduces the concept of 'higher of the two stations' (a'la al-manzilatayn). Withholding lutf exposes the servant to this higher reward, while giving lutf lowers him.
Even if God knows the servant won't act without lutf, the exposure itself is beneficial.
4
Analyze the analogy of respite
Compare to God granting respite to someone He knows will eventually disbelieve. Both granting respite and withholding lutf provide exposure to higher reward, even if the outcome is known.
This exposes the Mu'tazila's inconsistency in considering foreknowledge.
5
See the logical inconsistency
The Mu'tazila consider foreknowledge when demanding lutf but ignore it in the case of respite. The author concludes this is based on desire, not reason.
Ask yourself: why would they treat the two cases differently?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا منهم مناقضة عظيمة حيث أرجعوا اللطف على الله تعالى، وذلك أن الفعل يتصور حصوله بالتمكن بدون هذا اللطف على ما حكينا، ولا شك أن تحصيل الفعل بدون اللطف أشق على البدن وأكثر ثوابًا، وتحصيله مع اللطف أخف وأيسر على البدن فيكون أقل ثوابًا فكان الأصلح للعبد؟ والتعويض لأعلى المنزلتين أن يمنعه اللطف ليكون ثواب فعله أجزل والمنفعة به أوفر، وأكمل، وإن كان يعلم الله تعالى أنه لا يفعل بدون اللطف ولكن مع هذا منعه تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين وإعطائه إزالة له على أعلى المنزلتين إلى أدونهما، وهذا ليس يصلح كما أنه تعالى يمهل من يعلم أنه لو بلغ لكفر ومن يعلم أنه بعد ما زجى عمره في الإسلام ثمانين سنة يرتد بعد ذلك إذ هو بهذا الإمهال عرضة لأعلى المنزلتين، وإن كان يعلم أنه يكفر ويرتد فإن كانت العبرة لما يحصل من العاقبة المعلومة، وكان إعطاء اللطف أصلح إذ لو لم يعطه لما فعل لكان ينبغي أن لا يمهل هناك فإنه إن كان لا يمهل لا يكفر، وسقط التعريض على مصلحة يعلم أنها لا تحصل في الفصلين جميعًا، وإن كانت العبرة لنفس المصلحة وكان التعريض لها هو الأصلح، ولا عبرة للعلم بحصولها وعدم حصولها، كان منع اللطف هاهنا والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح فأما اعتبار العلم في باب إعطاء اللطف وإسقاط اعتباره في باب الإمهال إلى أن يكفر فبناء للأمر على تشهي النفس وميلان الهوى دون الدليل والعقل عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
This is a great contradiction from them, where they made lutf (grace) obligatory upon God Almighty. That is because the act can be conceived to occur through ability without this lutf, as we have related. There is no doubt that performing the act without lutf is harder on the body and more rewarding, while performing it with lutf is lighter and easier on the body and thus less rewarding. So the most beneficial for the servant and the compensation for the higher of the two stations is that He prevent lutf from him, so that the reward of his act is more abundant and the benefit is greater and more complete. Even if God Almighty knows that he will not act without lutf, nonetheless preventing him is an exposure to the higher of the two stations, and giving it to him is a removal from the higher of the two stations to the lower. And this is not proper, just as He Almighty grants respite to one whom He knows would disbelieve if he were to reach [maturity], and to one whom He knows that after spending eighty years of his life in Islam he will apostatize thereafter, for by this respite he is exposed to the higher of the two stations, even though He knows he will disbelieve and apostatize. For if the consideration is based on what results from the known outcome, and giving lutf is more beneficial since if He did not give it he would not act, then He should not have granted respite there, because if He does not grant respite he does not disbelieve, and the exposure to a benefit that is known not to occur falls in both cases. And if the consideration is based on the benefit itself, and exposure to it is more beneficial, with no regard to the knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence, then preventing lutf here and exposure to the higher of the two stations is more beneficial. As for considering knowledge in the matter of giving lutf while disregarding it in the matter of granting respite until he disbelieves, that is building the matter on whims of the soul and inclinations of passion, not on evidence and reason. May God Almighty protect us from that.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God is obligated to provide lutf (grace) to enable humans to obey. The author begins by identifying a great contradiction: the Mu'tazila make lutf obligatory upon God. He then explains that an act can be performed without such lutf through mere ability (tamakkun). Performing the act without lutf is harder on the body and thus yields greater reward; with lutf it is easier and yields less reward. Therefore, the most beneficial (aslah) for the servant and the compensation for the higher of the two stations (i.e., the highest reward) is that God withholds lutf, so that the servant's reward is greater. Even if God knows that without lutf the servant would not act, still withholding lutf gives the servant an opportunity to attain the higher station, while giving lutf would lower him. The author then draws an analogy with God granting respite to a person whom He knows will eventually disbelieve or apostatize: by granting respite, God exposes him to the possibility of the higher station. If the consideration were based solely on the known outcome, then giving lutf would be more beneficial (since without it the act wouldn't occur), but then God should not grant respite to potential disbelievers, because without respite they would not disbelieve, and the benefit of exposure would be lost in both cases. However, if the consideration is based on the benefit itself (the exposure to higher reward) regardless of foreknowledge, then withholding lutf is more beneficial. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila's selective use of foreknowledge (considering it in the case of lutf but ignoring it in the case of respite) is arbitrary, based on desire rather than reason.

Key Terms
اللطف
التعريض
أعلى المنزلتين
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to provide lutf (grace) to every servant because He is merciful.
Correction: The author argues that withholding lutf can be more beneficial for the servant, as it leads to greater reward, and God's mercy is not constrained by a simplistic obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is withholding lutf (grace) more beneficial for the servant?
A) Because without lutf, the servant's act is harder and thus yields greater reward.
B) Because God is not obligated to provide any help.
C) Because lutf is impossible for God to give.
D) Because the servant does not need lutf at all.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that God must provide lutf is contradictory because withholding it can yield greater reward, and their selective use of foreknowledge is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face difficulty in obeying God, do you secretly wish He had made it easier for you, or do you embrace the hardship as a sign that He wants greater reward for you? This lesson shows that withholding ease can be more beneficial. How does that challenge your assumptions about God's mercy?
?
Have you ever insisted that God must act in a certain way according to your sense of fairness, only to later realize you were projecting your own desires? The Mu'tazila are accused of building on 'whims of the soul.' Where in your own life might you be doing the same regarding God's attributes?
?
If you truly believed that God's foreknowledge does not limit His generosity—that He exposes you to good even when He knows you will fail—would you change how you approach moments of temptation or spiritual laziness? What specific step will you take this week to act, even when you feel no grace making it easy?

The Infidel and the Failure of Aslah
Nasafi argues that Mu'tazilite aslah fails because God cannot give more than He has to infidels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah fails when applied to the case of the infidel, and how Nasafi exposes its logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If God gave an infidel utmost benefit and he still disbelieves, then God cannot be said to have power to guide.

In this passage, Nasafi offers a decisive refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. He uses the stark example of the infidel to show that the Mu'tazilite position leads to a contradiction: if God has given every possible benefit and the infidel still rejects faith, then either God lacks the power to guide or the benefit was not enough. This short argument cuts to the heart of the aslah debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: كافر, أصلح, صلاح, فساد, مقدور.
The passage is concise, so read it twice.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Nasafi assumes for argument's sake that God gives the infidel the utmost of what is possible (the most beneficial). This is the Mu'tazilite position he will challenge.
3
Trace the logical consequence
If the infidel does not believe despite receiving that benefit, then according to Mu'tazilite reasoning, God does not possess the means to bring about goodness or reform. Ask yourself: why does this conclusion follow?
The key is that the Mu'tazilites define 'goodness' (salah) in a way that ties it to what God can do, leading to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شك أن كل كافر قد أعطاه الله تعالى عندهم غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح ولم يؤمن به الكافر فلم يكن على رأيهم الله تعالى ملك ما به الصلاح ولا قدرة على إصلاح من يشاء من عباده أن يصلح لو بذل جميع ما في خزائنه إذ ما من شيء يملك به الصلاح أو يقدر عليه إلا ويكون به على ذلك القدر فساده.
Then there is no doubt that every infidel – according to them – God has given him the utmost of what is within His capacity of the more beneficial (aslah), and the infidel did not believe. Therefore, according to their view, God does not possess that by which goodness (salah) is achieved, nor does He have power to reform whomever He wills of His servants to be reformed, even if He were to expend all that is in His treasuries. For there is nothing by which He possesses goodness or has power over it except that through that measure He has its corruption (fasad).
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a conclusive argument against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The author states that every infidel, according to Mu'tazilite theory, has been given by God the utmost of what is within His power of the most beneficial (aslah), yet the infidel still does not believe. This implies that God, in their view, does not possess the means to achieve goodness (salah) or the power to reform whom He wills, even if He exhausted all His treasures. The reason is that whatever God possesses of goodness or power, it is accompanied by its corruption (fasad) for that measure. In other words, the Mu'tazilite position leads to a contradiction: if God gave all possible benefit and the infidel still disbelieves, then either God lacks the ability to create faith, or the benefit given was insufficient. Thus, the doctrine of aslah is untenable.

Key Terms
كافر
أصلح
صلاح
فساد
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi is claiming that God cannot give benefit to infidels or that God is powerless.
Correction: Nasafi is not denying God's power; he is showing that the Mu'tazilite position leads to a contradiction: if God gave all possible benefit and the infidel remains in disbelief, then on their own terms, God lacks the power to create faith. This refutes their doctrine.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the Mu'tazilite view of aslah from the example of the infidel?
A) It proves that God gives faith to all infidels.
B) It shows that God lacks the means to reform whom He wills.
C) It confirms that the infidel is not accountable.
D) It demonstrates that the infidel deserves only punishment.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of aslah by showing that, if applied to the infidel, it would imply God lacks the power to guide, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the infidel received the utmost benefit from God and still disbelieved, what does that reveal about the nature of your own faith? Are you relying on worldly blessings to maintain your iman, or is your faith rooted solely in Allah's mercy and guidance?
?
Nasafi points out that every measure of goodness in this world carries with it a measure of corruption (fasad). Where in your life have you seen that even good things—wealth, health, relationships—can lead to spiritual harm? How will you protect your heart from that corruption today?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought God must always give the most beneficial, but the reality of the infidel's disbelief shatters that assumption. Are there assumptions you hold about what God 'should' do for you? Are you willing to submit to Allah's absolute wisdom, even when His giving or withholding seems contrary to your notion of what is best?

The Wisdom of Supplication: Refuting Two Objections
Nasafi refutes two Mu'tazilite objections on supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite objection that supplication must always yield benefit is invalid, and how divine wisdom operates beyond immediate utility.

Core Idea
Supplication is commanded universally; its wisdom is not tied to individual outcomes.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah), and therefore supplication should always result in benefit. Nasafi here addresses two objections raised by a hypothetical Mu'tazilite opponent. First, he refutes the claim that supplication only works if it leads to abandoning sin. Then, he tackles a subtler argument about praying for recovery from illness. As you read, notice how Nasafi shifts the ground from utility to divine command and the relativity of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the full objection and first response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the first objection (about sin prevention) and Nasafi's response. Notice that he says supplication is commanded universally, and benefit is not guaranteed for everyone.
The phrase 'must be outside wisdom' is key: God's wisdom does not require benefit for each individual.
2
Analyze the second objection on illness
The opponent shifts to a new argument: if illness is harmful, praying to remove it is good; if illness is beneficial, praying to remove it is bad. Nasafi counters by pointing out that the status of illness can change at the time of supplication.
Consider how the opponent assumes a static state, while Nasafi highlights the dynamic nature of benefit and harm.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about what this tells us about divine attributes and causality. God commands supplication, but its outcome depends on His wisdom, not on a requirement to always give the best in every situation.
This rebuts the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترض عليه فقال : إذا كان فيمن يدعو من يترك المعصية فقد حصلت له اللطيفة وصحت الفائدة في الدعاء. قيل له : الأمر بالدعاء عام في المكلفين وكانت المسألة حسنة من كل واحد من السائلين ويجب خروجه عن الحكمة فيمن لم يكن في مقدوره له لطيفة وذلك أكثر في العدد ممن حصلت له الفائدة. فانقطع وانتقل إلى ما لا يصح من الاعتراض، على أن هذا الاعتراض لا يستقيم في حق الدعاء ومسألة كشف ما به من المرض، فإن ذلك إن لم يكن مصلحة قد فعل ما ليس بمصلحة وإن كان مصلحة فلم أمر بمسألة دفع المصلحة؟ فإن قلت إنه عند الدعاء يصير مفسدة والمصلحة حينئذ تصير في دفعه وإثبات ضده الذي كان حتى الآن مفسدة. قلنا: لما أمر بالدعاء وفيه حصل ما هو من المصلحة مفسدة وجعل ما ليس فيه من المفسدة مصلحة ولو جعل كذلك أي منفعة لهذا السائل في ذلك الوقت وقد كان قبله في مصلحة والمفسدة عنه منتفية، والله الموفق.
So he objected and said: If among those who supplicate there is one who abandons disobedience, then the subtle benefit (al-latifa) has been obtained for him and the benefit in the supplication is sound. It was said to him: The command to supplicate is general for all morally responsible persons, and the request is good from every one of the petitioners, and it must be outside wisdom for one in whose capacity there is no subtle benefit, and such persons are more in number than those for whom benefit is obtained. So he was silenced and turned to an invalid objection. Moreover, this objection is not valid regarding supplication and the matter of requesting the removal of the illness afflicting him, for if that [illness] is not a source of benefit, then he has done what is not a benefit, and if it is a benefit, then why command the request to remove the benefit? If you say that at the time of supplication it becomes a source of harm and the benefit then lies in its removal and the establishment of its opposite which was up to now a source of harm. We said: Since He commanded supplication, and in it what was a benefit became a harm, and He made what was not a harm into a benefit, and if He made it so, what benefit is there for this petitioner at that time when before he was in benefit and harm was absent from him? And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair defending the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on supplication (du'a) against the Mu'tazilite view that God must always grant the most beneficial (al-aslah). The objector argues that supplication is beneficial only if it leads the supplicant to abandon sin. The author responds that the command to supplicate is universal; benefit is not guaranteed for every individual, but the act of supplication is good in itself. The objector then shifts to an invalid argument about requesting removal of illness: if illness is harmful, it is not a benefit; if it is beneficial, why ask to remove it? The author counters that at the time of supplication, the illness becomes harmful, and its removal becomes beneficial, but before supplication the person was already in a state of benefit (health) – so the change is due to God's wisdom, not a requirement that God always give the best in every case.

Key Terms
الدعاء
اللطيفة
المصلحة
المفسدة
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication is only beneficial if it directly prevents sin or improves one's state.
Correction: The command to supplicate is general, and the act itself is good regardless of outcome. Benefit is not a necessary condition for the wisdom of supplication.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the objector's argument about illness (asking to remove it) fail?
A) Because illness is never a benefit.
B) Because the command to supplicate is general and the objector ignored the change in circumstances.
C) Because removing illness is always harmful.
D) Because supplication is only for the righteous.

Key Takeaway
Supplication is a universal command; its wisdom lies in obedience, not in the guarantee of personal benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you supplicate, do you secretly believe that if your situation doesn't improve, your prayer is wasted? How does Nasafi's argument challenge that assumption, and what does that reveal about your view of God's wisdom?
?
In light of this lesson, examine your own du'a: Do you supplicate primarily to change your circumstances, or as an act of worship and submission to the One who commands it? What would it take for you to pray purely out of obedience, even without seeing a personal benefit?
?
What does your attitude toward unanswered prayers reveal about your trust in God's wisdom? Are you willing to accept that the command to supplicate is based on divine wisdom, not on your own perceived benefit, and how would that change your daily practice of du'a?

God Cannot Be Obligated to Give the Most Beneficial
Nasafi proves impossibility of the Mu'tazilite al-aslah via a two-aspect argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that obligating God to give the most beneficial (al-aslah) leads to contradiction: since what God gives does not actually reform the disbeliever, it is more corrupting, not the best.

Core Idea
Demanding the best from God backfires: what He gives is actually the worst for the disbeliever.

In this passage, Nasafi dismantles the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah by showing that even if God gives the utmost in His power, the disbeliever remains in disbelief. The very act of giving, when it does not produce faith, results in greater corruption and severer punishment. Nasafi then distills this into two clear aspects that expose the impossibility of obligating God to give what is 'most beneficial.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice how Nasafi begins with an indisputable fact: every disbeliever has already received the utmost benefit, yet still disbelieves.
2
Identify the core logical problem
Find where Nasafi says that what God possesses does not bring about righteousness, and that giving it is actually more corrupting. Understand why this refutes the Mu'tazilite demand.
3
Find the two summarized aspects
Locate the section where Nasafi says 'this statement is established from two aspects.' List them: (1) giving to one known to disbelieve is more corrupting; (2) since only corrupting things are in God's power, He cannot give the most beneficial.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the final sentence: obligating God to do what He has no power over is 'great ignorance.' Ask yourself how this aligns with the Mu'tazilite principle that God does not obligate beyond capacity.
This connects the argument to the broader kalam principle of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شك أن كل كافر قد أعطاه الله تعالى عندهم غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح ولم يؤمن به الكافر فلم يكن على رأيهم الله تعالى ملك ما به الصلاح ولا قدرة على إصلاح من يشاء من عباده أن يصلح لو بذل جميع ما في خزائنه إذ ما من شيء يملك به الصلاح أو يقدر عليه إلا ويكون به على ذلك القدر فساده، ثم لا يصلح أحد به فكيف يحتمل إلزام ما لا يملكه ولا يقوى عليه إذ ما يملكه هو لا يحصل به الصلاح، وما لا يحصل به الصلاح يكون أصلح من غيره لاستوائهما في أن لا أثر لهما في تحصيل الصلاح بل إعطائه يكون أفسد؛ إذ كفره وعصيانه معه أقبح والعقوبة عليه أغلظ؛ إذ العصيان مع توفر الزواجر وترك الطاعة مع كثرة النعم والدواعي أقبح واستيجاب العقوبة عليه أقوى فبعد ذلك تقرر هذا الكلام من وجهين أحدهما أن إعطاء من علم أنه لا يؤمن يكون أفسد له على ما قررنا، وقد أعطاه فإذا فعل به ما هو الأفسد لا ما هو الأصلح. والثاني: أن ذلك إذا كان على هذا التقرير إفسادًا لا إصلاحًا وليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا، فلم يكن إذا في مقدوره الأصلح بل في مقدوره الأفسد، فإيجاب المعتزلة إياه ما لا قدرة له على تحصيله مع قولهم أن ليس الله تعالى ولاية تكليف العبد ما لا قدرة له عليه جهل عظيم. ومما يدل على بطلان قول المعتزلة : إن القول بأن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى شيء يتعلق به صلاح الكافر والعاصي وراء ما فعل بكل واحد من الكفرة والعصاة قول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى والقول به كفر فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول لما في مقدوره نهاية بل نزعم أنه ليس لما عند الله مما فيه الصلاح غاية ولا نهاية، وأن في سلطانه وقدرته وخزائن رحمته من أمثال ما فعل هو أصلح لهم مما لا غاية له ولا نهاية، والله جل ذكره إنما مما هو بهم يفعل بهم من ذلك ويعطيهم منه في كل وقت مقدار حاجتهم وما يعلم أنه أصلح لهم
Then there is no doubt that every disbeliever has been given by God Almighty, according to them, the utmost of what is in His power of the most beneficial (al-aslah), and the disbeliever did not believe. So according to their view, God Almighty does not possess that by which there is righteousness, nor has He power to reform whomever He wills of His servants, even if He expended all that is in His treasuries. For there is no thing by which He possesses righteousness or has power over it except that there is corruption in that measure. Then no one is set right by it, so how can He be obligated to give what He does not possess and has no power over? For what He possesses does not bring about righteousness, and what does not bring about righteousness cannot be more beneficial than another, since they are equal in having no effect in attaining righteousness. Rather, giving it is more corrupting, for his disbelief and disobedience with it are worse, and the punishment for it is more severe. For disobedience with abundant deterrents and abandonment of obedience with abundant blessings and incentives is worse, and deserving of punishment is stronger. After that, this statement is established from two aspects: One is that giving to one whom He knows will not believe is more corrupting for him, as we have stated, and He gave him. So if He does to him what is more corrupting, not what is most beneficial. The second is that if this is according to this analysis a corruption, not a reform, and in God's power there is nothing except this, then in His power there is not the most beneficial, but rather the more corrupting. So the Mu'tazila's obligating Him to do what He has no power to accomplish, while they say that God does not have the authority to obligate a servant with what he has no power over, is great ignorance. And among what indicates the invalidity of the Mu'tazila's statement is that the statement that there is nothing in God's power that pertains to the righteousness of the disbeliever and the disobedient beyond what He has done to each of the disbelievers and disobedient is a statement of the finitude of God's powers, and to say so is disbelief. Then if they say: We do not say that there is a limit to what is in His power, rather we claim that there is no limit and no end to what God has that contains righteousness, and that in His dominion, power, and treasuries of mercy there are things like what He did that are more beneficial for them, with no limit or end, and God—glorious is His mention—only does to them from that and gives them from it at every time the amount of their need and what He knows is most beneficial for them.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step proof against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The author first asserts that every disbeliever has already received from God the utmost possible benefit, yet they still disbelieve. Therefore, according to the Mu'tazila's own principle, God does not possess anything that could actually reform them, because if He did, He would have given it. The author then argues that what God does give does not produce righteousness, so it cannot be called more beneficial—it is actually more corrupting, as it increases guilt and punishment. The argument is then summarized in two points: (1) Giving to one known to be a disbeliever is more corrupting, so God gives what is worse, not the best; (2) If all God can give is corrupting, then His power contains only corruption, not the most beneficial, so the Mu'tazila's demand is impossible. The author then adds another proof: the Mu'tazilite claim that God has not given any benefit beyond what He has done implies that God's power is finite—a statement of kufr (disbelief). The passage concludes with the Mu'tazila's anticipated objection that they do not claim finitude but rather that God has infinite beneficial things and only gives according to need. (The author's refutation of this objection would follow on the next page, but the window ends here.)

Key Terms
الأصلح
المقدور
الفساد
الإصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's two aspects, what is the conclusion about God's giving to the disbeliever?
A) God gives the most beneficial to every disbeliever.
B) God gives what is more corrupting, not the most beneficial, and cannot do otherwise.
C) God gives what the disbeliever deserves.
D) God's power contains both benefit and harm.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite demand that God give the most beneficial fails because what God actually gives to disbelievers is corrupting, not reforming, and thus cannot be the best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or disappointment, do you ever find yourself silently accusing God of not giving you what is 'best'? After reading this lesson, how will you reframe your expectations of God's choices for you?
?
This lesson shows that even abundant blessings can become a means of greater corruption for those who reject faith. Are there blessings in your life that have made you more careless or distanced you from gratitude? What will you do to change that today?
?
The Mu'tazilites assumed they knew what justice requires of God. In what areas of your life do you still think you know better than God what should happen? Will you surrender that judgment to Him, even if it feels unfair?

The Finitude of Divine Power and the Mu'tazilite Objection
Nasafi presents finitude argument and Mu'tazilite objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's second argument that the Mu'tazilite position implies finite divine power (kufr), and the Mu'tazilite objection that God has infinite beneficial things.

Core Idea
Claiming God's power is limited is kufr; the Mu'tazila try to avoid this by asserting infinite benefit.

In the second part of this refutation, Nasafi presents a new angle: the Mu'tazilite doctrine leads to the statement that God's power is finite — a claim of kufr. He then anticipates a likely Mu'tazilite objection: they do not mean finitude but rather that God has infinite beneficial resources and gives according to need. The passage ends mid-objection, setting up the next part of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the new argument
Read the Arabic and translation from 'ومما يدل' to 'أصلح لهم'. Notice the shift: now Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazilite position implies a limit on God's power.
2
Understand why finitude is kufr
Reflect on why saying God's power is finite (تناهي المقدورات) is considered disbelief (كفر). In Islamic theology, God's power is absolute and unlimited.
This is a serious charge — the Mu'tazila must avoid it.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazilite objection
Read the objection: 'We do not say there is a limit... rather we claim there is no end to the beneficial things in God's power.' Consider how they attempt to escape the charge of finitude.
The passage ends here; the refutation is not included in this chunk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شك أن كل كافر قد أعطاه الله تعالى عندهم غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح ولم يؤمن به الكافر فلم يكن على رأيهم الله تعالى ملك ما به الصلاح ولا قدرة على إصلاح من يشاء من عباده أن يصلح لو بذل جميع ما في خزائنه إذ ما من شيء يملك به الصلاح أو يقدر عليه إلا ويكون به على ذلك القدر فساده، ثم لا يصلح أحد به فكيف يحتمل إلزام ما لا يملكه ولا يقوى عليه إذ ما يملكه هو لا يحصل به الصلاح، وما لا يحصل به الصلاح يكون أصلح من غيره لاستوائهما في أن لا أثر لهما في تحصيل الصلاح بل إعطائه يكون أفسد؛ إذ كفره وعصيانه معه أقبح والعقوبة عليه أغلظ؛ إذ العصيان مع توفر الزواجر وترك الطاعة مع كثرة النعم والدواعي أقبح واستيجاب العقوبة عليه أقوى فبعد ذلك تقرر هذا الكلام من وجهين أحدهما أن إعطاء من علم أنه لا يؤمن يكون أفسد له على ما قررنا، وقد أعطاه فإذا فعل به ما هو الأفسد لا ما هو الأصلح. والثاني: أن ذلك إذا كان على هذا التقرير إفسادًا لا إصلاحًا وليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا، فلم يكن إذا في مقدوره الأصلح بل في مقدوره الأفسد، فإيجاب المعتزلة إياه ما لا قدرة له على تحصيله مع قولهم أن ليس الله تعالى ولاية تكليف العبد ما لا قدرة له عليه جهل عظيم. ومما يدل على بطلان قول المعتزلة : إن القول بأن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى شيء يتعلق به صلاح الكافر والعاصي وراء ما فعل بكل واحد من الكفرة والعصاة قول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى والقول به كفر فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول لما في مقدوره نهاية بل نزعم أنه ليس لما عند الله مما فيه الصلاح غاية ولا نهاية، وأن في سلطانه وقدرته وخزائن رحمته من أمثال ما فعل هو أصلح لهم مما لا غاية له ولا نهاية، والله جل ذكره إنما مما هو بهم يفعل بهم من ذلك ويعطيهم منه في كل وقت مقدار حاجتهم وما يعلم أنه أصلح لهم
Then there is no doubt that every disbeliever has been given by God Almighty, according to them, the utmost of what is in His power of the most beneficial (al-aslah), and the disbeliever did not believe. So according to their view, God Almighty does not possess that by which there is righteousness, nor has He power to reform whomever He wills of His servants, even if He expended all that is in His treasuries. For there is no thing by which He possesses righteousness or has power over it except that there is corruption in that measure. Then no one is set right by it, so how can He be obligated to give what He does not possess and has no power over? For what He possesses does not bring about righteousness, and what does not bring about righteousness cannot be more beneficial than another, since they are equal in having no effect in attaining righteousness. Rather, giving it is more corrupting, for his disbelief and disobedience with it are worse, and the punishment for it is more severe. For disobedience with abundant deterrents and abandonment of obedience with abundant blessings and incentives is worse, and deserving of punishment is stronger. After that, this statement is established from two aspects: One is that giving to one whom He knows will not believe is more corrupting for him, as we have stated, and He gave him. So if He does to him what is more corrupting, not what is most beneficial. The second is that if this is according to this analysis a corruption, not a reform, and in God's power there is nothing except this, then in His power there is not the most beneficial, but rather the more corrupting. So the Mu'tazila's obligating Him to do what He has no power to accomplish, while they say that God does not have the authority to obligate a servant with what he has no power over, is great ignorance. And among what indicates the invalidity of the Mu'tazila's statement is that the statement that there is nothing in God's power that pertains to the righteousness of the disbeliever and the disobedient beyond what He has done to each of the disbelievers and disobedient is a statement of the finitude of God's powers, and to say so is disbelief. Then if they say: We do not say that there is a limit to what is in His power, rather we claim that there is no limit and no end to what God has that contains righteousness, and that in His dominion, power, and treasuries of mercy there are things like what He did that are more beneficial for them, with no limit or end, and God—glorious is His mention—only does to them from that and gives them from it at every time the amount of their need and what He knows is most beneficial for them.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step proof against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The author first asserts that every disbeliever has already received from God the utmost possible benefit, yet they still disbelieve. Therefore, according to the Mu'tazila's own principle, God does not possess anything that could actually reform them, because if He did, He would have given it. The author then argues that what God does give does not produce righteousness, so it cannot be called more beneficial—it is actually more corrupting, as it increases guilt and punishment. The argument is then summarized in two points: (1) Giving to one known to be a disbeliever is more corrupting, so God gives what is worse, not the best; (2) If all God can give is corrupting, then His power contains only corruption, not the most beneficial, so the Mu'tazila's demand is impossible. The author then adds another proof: the Mu'tazilite claim that God has not given any benefit beyond what He has done implies that God's power is finite—a statement of kufr (disbelief). The passage concludes with the Mu'tazila's anticipated objection that they do not claim finitude but rather that God has infinite beneficial things and only gives according to need. (The author's refutation of this objection would follow on the next page, but the window ends here.)

Key Terms
تناهي المقدورات
الأصلح
الخزائن

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi claim is implied by the Mu'tazilite statement that there is nothing in God's power beyond what He has done for disbelievers?
A) That God's mercy is infinite.
B) That God's power is finite (تناهي المقدورات).
C) That God is unjust.
D) That God does not care about disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position leads to the unacceptable conclusion that God's power is finite, a form of disbelief, and their objection about infinite benefit does not resolve the logical problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you ever find yourself tempted to think that God's power is limited by what you see as 'fair' or 'beneficial'? This lesson exposes the Mu'tazilite claim as leading to kufr. Where in your heart do you subtly demand that God act according to your standard of what is best? Will you repent of that and submit to His absolute power, even when you do not understand His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that God only gives what is needed and most beneficial, but this lesson shows that implies a limit on His power. When you face hardships or see the apparent 'injustice' of the disbeliever's punishment, do you trust that God's power is infinite and that His action is not constrained by your understanding? What would it mean to truly believe that God's power has no limit—even in matters that seem unfair to you?
?
This lesson warns that saying God's power is finite is kufr. Have you ever, even implicitly, questioned God's ability to do what He wills, based on your limited perspective? How can you train yourself to immediately reject any thought that limits God's power, and instead say 'Allah is able over all things' with certainty?

Giving More to the Disbeliever is Harmful, Not Beneficial
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ with a two-part argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why giving increased blessings to those who persist in disbelief is actually more corrupting (afṣad) and not the most beneficial (aṣlaḥ), according to Nasafi's refutation of the Muʿtazila doctrine.

Core Idea
More blessings can increase sin and punishment, so they are not always beneficial.

The Muʿtazila held that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His servants. But Nasafi challenges this by considering those who will never believe: giving them more only amplifies their sin and due punishment. This passage presents a crisp argument that such giving is actually more corrupting (afṣad), thereby undermining the Muʿtazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Let the structure of Nasafi's argument emerge naturally.
Pay special attention to the two 'فوجهين' (two aspects) he lists.
2
Identify the premise
Notice that Nasafi starts with a general statement about reform and then applies it to the case of a disbeliever: giving more leads to uglier sin and heavier punishment.
3
Examine the two arguments
Nasafi presents two formal arguments. First, giving to one known to disbelieve is more corrupting (afṣad). Second, if only that corrupting act is in God's power, then the more beneficial is not in His power. See how both lead to the same conclusion.
These two correspond to الوجه الأول and الوجه الثاني in the Arabic.
4
Understand the implication for Mu'tazila
Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila's demand for al-aṣlaḥ is self-contradictory: they insist on something God cannot do, yet they deny that God burdens beyond capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلح أحد به فكيف يحتمل إلزام ما لا يملكه ولا يقوى عليه إذ ما يملكه هو لا يحصل به الصلاح، وما لا يحصل به الصلاح يكون أصلح من غيره لاستوائهما في أن لا أثر لهما في تحصيل الصلاح بل إعطائه يكون أفسد ؛ إذ كفره وعصيانه معه أقبح والعقوبة عليه أغلظ؛ إذ العصيان مع توفر الزواجر وترك الطاعة مع كثرة النعم والدواعي أقبح واستيجاب العقوبة عليه أقوى فبعد ذلك تقرر هذا الكلام من وجهين أحدهما أن إعطاء من علم أنه لا يؤمن يكون أفسد له على ما قررنا، وقد أعطاه فإذا فعل به ما هو الأفسد لا ما هو الأصلح. والثاني: أن ذلك إذا كان على هذا التقرير إفسادًا لا إصلاحًا وليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا، فلم يكن إذا في مقدوره الأصلح بل في مقدوره الأفسد، فإيجاب المعتزلة إياه ما لا قدرة له على تحصيله مع قولهم أن ليس الله تعالى ولاية تكليف العبد ما لا قدرة له عليه جهل عظيم.
One is reformed by it, so how can he be compelled to what he does not possess and cannot do? For what he possesses does not bring about reform, and what does not bring about reform is not more beneficial than another because they are equal in having no effect in achieving reform; rather, giving it is more corrupting, since his disbelief and disobedience with it are uglier and the punishment harsher, because disobedience with abundant deterrents and abandonment of obedience with plentiful blessings and incentives is uglier and the deserving of punishment stronger. After that, this statement is established in two respects: First, that giving to one whom it is known will not believe is more corrupting for him, as we have decided, and He gave him, so He did to him what is more corrupting, not what is more beneficial. Second, that if this is on this basis corruption and not reform, and in the power of Allah the Exalted there is nothing but this, then there is in His power the more beneficial, but rather in His power the more corrupting. So the Mu'tazila's imposing upon Him what He has no power to attain, along with their statement that Allah the Exalted does not have the authority to impose on the servant what he cannot do, is immense ignorance.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) is self-defeating. The author begins by establishing that giving additional blessings to someone known to remain a disbeliever is actually more corrupting (afṣad) for him, because it increases his sin and the due punishment. He then presents two arguments. First, giving to one known to disbelieve is more corrupting, not more beneficial, so what God did is the more corrupting, contradicting the claim that God must do the most beneficial. Second, if only that corrupting act is in God's power, then the more beneficial is not in His power, so the Mu'tazila are demanding something impossible, which is absurd given their own principle that God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aṣlaḥ)
الأفسد (al-afṣad)
الصلاح (al-ṣalāḥ)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Giving more to a disbeliever is always beneficial.
Correction: It can increase sin and punishment, making it more corrupting (afṣad) rather than beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is giving more to a disbeliever not al-aslah?
A) Because the disbeliever does not deserve it.
B) Because it increases his sin and punishment, making it more corrupting.
C) Because God cannot give more.
D) Because the disbeliever will reject it.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that giving more blessings to a disbeliever is more corrupting, not more beneficial, refuting the Muʿtazila doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that more worldly blessings mean God is pleased with you? How does this passage—showing that blessings can increase sin and punishment for the disbeliever—challenge that assumption, and what will you do to check your own heart's state instead of relying on outward favors?
?
Consider a blessing in your life right now that you might be using carelessly or in a way that distracts you from obedience. How does Nasafi's argument that 'abandonment of obedience with plentiful blessings is uglier' confront you, and what specific step will you take today to turn that blessing into a means of drawing closer to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila thought God must always do the most beneficial, but this passage says that sometimes what God does is 'more corrupting' for some. Are you willing to trust that God's withholding of some blessings is actually a mercy, even when you desperately want them? How will you practice this trust starting now?

The Implication of Denying God's Power to Benefit Disbelievers
Nasafi argues that denying God's power to benefit disbelievers implies finite power, which is kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila position on al-aslah logically leads to denying God's infinite power, and why this is considered kufr according to Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Denying God's ability to do more for disbelievers implies His power is finite—which is kufr.

The Mu'tazila argue that God must do the most beneficial for every person, yet they also claim that for disbelievers and sinners, God has not done what is more beneficial. Nasafi points out the logical contradiction: if God cannot do anything more beneficial for them, then His power is limited. This passage shows how a seemingly subtle theological position can lead to an unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Nasafi structures the argument concisely.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains a complete logical refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that there is nothing in God's power that pertains to the benefit of the disbeliever or sinner beyond what He has done.
This is the claim Nasafi is refuting.
3
Understand the logical implication
Nasafi says this claim implies that God's possibilities (maqdūrāt) are finite (tanāhī). Ask yourself: why does denying more beneficial acts lead to finite power?
If God cannot do something more beneficial, then His power to do beneficial acts is bounded.
4
Reflect on the gravity of the conclusion
Nasafi declares that saying such a thing is kufr (disbelief). Consider why this is so serious in Maturidi theology.
Affirming God's infinite power is essential; limiting it contradicts His omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على بطلان قول المعتزلة : إن القول بأن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى شيء يتعلق به صلاح الكافر والعاصي وراء ما فعل بكل واحد من الكفرة والعصاة قول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى والقول به کفر
And among what indicates the invalidity of the Mu'tazila's statement: that the saying that there is nothing in the power of Allah the Exalted that pertains to the benefit of the disbeliever and sinner beyond what He has done with each of the disbelievers and sinners is a statement of the finitude of the possibilities of Allah the Exalted, and saying it is kufr (disbelief).
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof that the Mu'tazila position on al-aslah leads to an unacceptable conclusion. The Mu'tazila claim that God must do the most beneficial for every person, but they also hold that for disbelievers and sinners, God has not done what is more beneficial than what He actually did. This implies that God cannot do anything more beneficial for them, which means His power has a limit. The author states that such a statement amounts to saying that God's power is finite (tanāhī al-maqdūrāt), and declaring that is an act of disbelief (kufr). Thus, the Mu'tazila doctrine is refuted by its own logical consequence.

Key Terms
تناهي المقدورات (tanāhī al-maqdūrāt)
كفر (kufr)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
صلاح (ṣalāḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Mu'tazila view on al-aslah is just a permissible theological opinion among Muslims.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly declares this view to be kufr because it implies that God's power is finite, which contradicts essential Islamic teachings.

Checkpoint

A) It is a permissible theological opinion.
B) It implies that God's power is finite, which is kufr.
C) It means the disbeliever is beyond reform.
D) It shows God's mercy.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God can do anything more beneficial for disbelievers logically leads to limiting God's power, which is an act of disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, believed that Allah cannot or will not bring benefit to someone because of their sins? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your belief in Allah's absolute power? Today, when you see a person you consider a sinner, pause and internally affirm that Allah's power over their situation is unlimited.
?
This lesson declares that limiting Allah's power is kufr. Examine your heart: do you sometimes feel that Allah's mercy or help is 'exhausted' for certain people? What concrete step will you take to replace such thoughts with the conviction that Allah's power is infinite and not bound by your reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila used their intellect to restrict what Allah can do. In what areas of your life have you placed subtle restrictions on Allah, assuming He cannot change a situation because of circumstances? Write down one area and consciously surrender it to Allah's unlimited power today.

Refuting the Mu'tazila Claim of Unlimited Beneficial Things
Nasafi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila objection with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God has unlimited beneficial things by exposing a dilemma that leads to attributing impotence to Allah.

Core Idea
Unlimited divine benefits create a dilemma: either God prevents benefit or lacks power.

The Mu'tazila argue that God has endless beneficial things but only gives what is needed. Nasafi exposes the flaw: if giving more would not cause belief, then God is powerless to grant that benefit. He uses a dilemma and an analogy to show that the Mu'tazila position inadvertently attributes impotence to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Mu'tazila claim that God has unlimited beneficial things but only gives according to need.
Notice they assert no limit to God's power over beneficial things.
2
Understand the author's framing
Nasafi attributes the objection to al-Ka'bi to expose their deception. Reflect on why he mentions him.
Al-Ka'bi is a prominent Mu'tazili imam.
3
Follow the first dilemma
If God gave more to the disbeliever, would he believe? If yes, God prevented his benefit. If no, God cannot make him believe.
This shows the Mu'tazila cannot escape both horns.
4
Trace the lutf extension
Nasafi extends the argument: denying God's power over a grace that would cause belief is like denying power to move what He knows He will not move.
This parallels the original dilemma.
5
Reflect on the outcome
The Mu'tazila position leads to describing God with impotence, contradicting their own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول لما في مقدوره نهاية بل نزعم أنه ليس لما عند الله مما فيه الصلاح غاية ولا نهاية، وأن في سلطانه وقدرته وخزائن رحمته من أمثال ما فعل هو أصلح لهم مما لا غاية له ولا نهاية، والله جل ذكره إنما مما هو بهم يفعل بهم من ذلك ويعطيهم منه في كل وقت مقدار حاجتهم وما يعلم أنه أصلح لهم وأدعى إلى الطاعة وأزجر عن المعصية، ذكرت السؤال بلفظ الكعبي وهو عندهم إمام أهل الأرض ليعلم المتأمل تمويههم في كل ما يتكلمون به، وزوغانهم عن محز الكلام والاشتغال بالتلبيس على العوام. فأقول في جوابه وبالله التوفيق -: إن كان في قدرة الله تعالى وخزائن رحمته أمثال ما فعل من الأصلح فما قولك لو فعل ذلك في الحال بهذا الكافر هل يؤمن؟ فإن قال : نعم. فقد أقر بمنع الأصلح إذ لم يفعل ذلك كله ليؤمن، وإن قال : لا قيل فإذا لا قدرة له على ما به لو آمن من هذا العبد فحصل له صلاحه، فإذا السؤال أنه هل يقدر أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا الذي فعل أم لا يصفه بالقدرة عليه؟ فينبغي أن يجيب عن هذا الحرف أو يعترض عليه، وإذا لم يكن عندك في مقدوره أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا فهذا هو آية النفاد والعجز عن تحصيل المراد. ثم يقال له : أليس من زعم أن الله تعالى يقدر أن يفعل في الوقت صلاحًا هو أصلح الأشياء للإنسان ولا يوصف بالقدرة على مثله يكون معجزا ربه؟ فلا بد من بلي. فقيل له: فإنك وإن أثبت قدرة على أمثال ما فعل فإذا لم تصفه بالقدرة على ما هو أصلح له أصلح له من هذا وصفته بالعجز أيضا ، ثم يقال له أليس أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه يؤمن واصفًا له بالعجز؟ فإذا قال نعم قيل فكذا من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه لا يؤمن به يكون واصفا له بالعجز كما أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه لا يحركه يكون واصفًا له بالعجز كمن لا يصفه بالقدرة على تحريك
If they say: 'We do not say that what is in His power has an end; rather we claim that there is no limit or end to what is with Allah of beneficial things, and that in His dominion, power, and treasures of mercy are examples like what He did that are more beneficial for them without limit or end, and Allah, exalted be His mention, only does to them from that and gives them from it at each time the amount of their need and what He knows is more beneficial for them and more conducive to obedience and more deterrent from disobedience' – I mentioned the question in the wording of al-Ka'bi, who is according to them the imam of the people of the earth, so that the contemplative person may know their obfuscation in everything they speak, and their deviation from the core of speech and their occupation with deception of the common people. I say in response, and with Allah is success: If in the power of Allah the Exalted and the treasures of His mercy are examples like what He did of the more beneficial, then what do you say – if He did that now to this disbeliever, would he believe? If he says 'Yes,' then he has confessed the prevention of the more beneficial, since He did not do all of that so that he would believe. And if he says 'No,' then it is said: Then He has no power over that by which this servant would believe and thus attain his benefit. So the question is: either He is able to do for him at the time what is more beneficial than this which He did, or He is not – do you ascribe power to Him? He should answer this point or object to it. And if you do not consider that in His power to do for him at the time something more beneficial than this, then this is a sign of exhaustion and inability to accomplish the intended. Then it is said to him: Is it not the case that whoever claims that Allah the Exalted is able to do at the time a benefit that is the most beneficial thing for the person, yet does not ascribe power to Him over its like, would be attributing impotence to his Lord? There is no escape from [answering]. So it is said to him: Even if you affirm power over examples like what He did, if you do not ascribe to Him power over what is more beneficial for him than this, you are also describing Him with impotence. Then it is said to him: Is it not that whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power over a grace by which one who is known to believe will believe is describing Him with impotence? If he says 'Yes,' then it is said: Likewise, whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power over a grace by which one who is known not to believe would believe is describing Him with impotence, just as whoever does not ascribe to Allah the Exalted power to move one whom He knows He will not move is describing Him with impotence, like one who does not ascribe to Him power to move.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazila objection and the author's detailed refutation. The Mu'tazila first object that they do not claim God's power has an end; rather, God has unlimited beneficial things but only gives what is needed. The author notes that he phrased the question according to al-Ka'bi (a prominent Mu'tazili) to expose their deception. Then he presents a dilemma: if God gave more benefits to the disbeliever, would he believe? If yes, then God prevented his benefit by not giving, which negates al-aslah. If no, then God cannot do it, showing impotence. The author then extends the argument: denying God's power to do a specific grace (luṭf) that would cause belief (even if the person is known not to believe) is equivalent to denying God's power to move what He knows He will not move; both attribute impotence to God. Thus, the Mu'tazila position is refuted by their own logic.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aṣlaḥ)
اللطف (al-luṭf)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
الكعبي (al-Kaʿbī)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila objection seems reasonable because God indeed has unlimited power and knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi shows the objection leads to attributing impotence to God, either by preventing what is beneficial or by lacking power to cause belief.

Checkpoint
In refuting the Mu'tazila, what does Nasafi say would follow if God gave more to the disbeliever and he still did not believe?
A) God prevented his benefit.
B) God has no power to make him believe.
C) The disbeliever is excused.
D) It proves al-aslah is correct.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their claim of unlimited benefits leads to a dilemma that ultimately attributes impotence to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that Allah has not given you something you desperately wanted, do you ever slip into thinking that He could have done better? How does this refutation expose the hidden danger in that thought?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's power encompasses everything, including what you think would be best for you, would you still complain when trials come? What specific disposition does this passage demand you cultivate?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed unlimited beneficence, but their logic led to blasphemy. Are there any areas in your life where your reasoning about Allah's actions is secretly rooted in your own measure of 'good' rather than submission to His absolute wisdom and power? Will you repent of that today?
